0.1\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="704" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg/220px-NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg/330px-NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg/440px-NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NL_Pop._by_Religion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As of  2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Christians were approximately 93% of the population of Newfoundland and Labrador.</div></div></div>\n<p>The largest single religious denomination by number of adherents according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canada_2011_Census" title="Canada 2011 Census">2011 National Household Survey</a> was the Roman Catholic Church, at 35.8% of the province\'s population (181,590 members). The major Protestant denominations made up 57.3% of the population, with the largest groups being the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church_of_Canada" title="Anglican Church of Canada">Anglican Church of Canada</a> at 25.1% of the total population (127,255 members), the <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Canada" title="United Church of Canada">United Church of Canada</a> at 15.5% (78,380 members), and the <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostal</a> churches at 6.5% (33,195 members), with other Protestant denominations in much smaller numbers. Non-Christians constituted only 6.8% of the population, with the majority of those respondents indicating "no religious affiliation" (6.2% of the total population).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_Profile,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador,_2011_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Profile,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador,_2011-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canada_2001_Census" title="Canada 2001 Census">2001 Canadian census</a>, the largest ethnic group in Newfoundland and Labrador is English (39.4%), followed by Irish (19.7%), Scots (6.0%), <a href="/wiki/Franco-Newfoundlander" title="Franco-Newfoundlander">French</a> (5.5%), and <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> (3.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> While half of all respondents also identified their ethnicity as "Canadian," 38% report their ethnicity as "Newfoundlander" in a 2003 <a href="/wiki/Statistics_Canada" title="Statistics Canada">Statistics Canada</a> Ethnic Diversity Survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More than 100,000 Newfoundlanders have applied for membership in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qalipu_Mi%27kmaq_First_Nation_Band" title="Qalipu Mi\'kmaq First Nation Band">Qalipu Mi\'kmaq First Nation Band</a>, equivalent to one-fifth of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>All currency is in <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">Canadian dollars</a>.</i>\n</p><p>For many years, Newfoundland and Labrador had experienced a depressed economy. Following <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Atlantic_northwest_cod_fishery" title="Collapse of the Atlantic northwest cod fishery">the collapse of the cod fishery</a> during the early 1990s, the province suffered record unemployment rates and the population decreased by roughly 60,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-population_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-population-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Due to a major energy and resources boom, the provincial economy has had a major turnaround since the turn of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Unemployment rates decreased, the population stabilized and had moderate growth. The province has gained record surpluses, which has rid it of its status as a "have not" province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review2011_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-review2011-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1076" data-file-width="2046" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg/220px-StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg/330px-StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg/440px-StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StJohns_Newfoundland_ViewfromSIgnalHill2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of St. John\'s from <a href="/wiki/Signal_Hill,_St._John%27s" title="Signal Hill, St. John\'s">Signal Hill</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Economic growth, gross domestic product (GDP), exports and employment resumed in 2010, after suffering the impacts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" title="Late-2000s recession">late-2000s recession</a>. In 2010, total capital investment in the province grew to $6.2&#160;billion, an increase of 23.0% compared to 2009. 2010 GDP reached $28.1&#160;billion, compared to $25.0&#160;billion in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review_2010_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-review_2010-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_sector_of_economic_activity" title="Tertiary sector of economic activity">Service industries</a> accounted for the largest share of GDP, especially financial services, health care and public administration. Other significant industries are mining, oil production and manufacturing. The total workforce in 2010 was 263,800 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-econ-103">[103]</a></sup> Per capita GDP in 2008 was $61,763, higher than the national average and third only to Alberta and Saskatchewan out of Canadian provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mines in Labrador, the iron ore mine at <a href="/wiki/Wabush" title="Wabush">Wabush</a>/<a href="/wiki/Labrador_City" title="Labrador City">Labrador City</a>, and the nickel mine in <a href="/wiki/Voisey%27s_Bay" title="Voisey\'s Bay">Voisey\'s Bay</a> produced a total of $3.3&#160;billion worth of ore in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review_2010_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-review_2010-101">[101]</a></sup> A mine at Duck Pond (30&#160;km (18&#160;mi) south of the now-closed mine at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buchans,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Buchans, Newfoundland and Labrador">Buchans</a>), started producing copper, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, silver and gold in 2007, and prospecting for new ore bodies continues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Mining accounted for 3.5% of the provincial GDP in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-econ-103">[103]</a></sup> The province produces 55% of Canada\'s total <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cef_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-cef-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quarry" title="Quarry">Quarries</a> producing <a href="/wiki/Dimension_stone" title="Dimension stone">dimension stone</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Slate" title="Slate">slate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>, account for less than $10&#160;million worth of material per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">Oil production</a> from offshore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_platforms" title="Oil platforms">oil platforms</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hibernia_(oil_field)" title="Hibernia (oil field)">Hibernia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Rose_(oil_field)" title="White Rose (oil field)">White Rose</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terra_Nova_(oil_field)" title="Terra Nova (oil field)">Terra Nova</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_fields" title="Oil fields">oil fields</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Banks" title="Grand Banks">Grand Banks</a> was of 110,000,000 barrels (17,000,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), which contributed to more than 15% of the province\'s GDP in 2006. Total production from the Hibernia field from 1997 to 2006 was 733,000,000 barrels (116,500,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) with an estimated value of $36&#160;billion. This will increase with the inclusion of the latest project, Hebron. Remaining reserves are estimated at almost 2 billion barrels (320<span style="margin-left:0.2em">&#215;<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) as of December 31, 2006. Exploration for new reserves is ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-econ-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TerraNovaOil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/TerraNovaOil.jpg/220px-TerraNovaOil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/TerraNovaOil.jpg/330px-TerraNovaOil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/TerraNovaOil.jpg/440px-TerraNovaOil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TerraNovaOil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Terra Nova <a href="/wiki/Floating_production_storage_and_offloading" title="Floating production storage and offloading">floating production storage and offloading</a> Vessel located in the <a href="/wiki/Terra_Nova_oil_field" title="Terra Nova oil field">Terra Nova oil field</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On June 16, 2009, provincial premier <a href="/wiki/Danny_Williams_(politician)" title="Danny Williams (politician)">Danny Williams</a> announced a tentative agreement to expand the <a href="/wiki/Hibernia_oil_field" title="Hibernia oil field">Hibernia oil field</a>. The government negotiated a 10-per-cent equity stake in the Hibernia South expansion, which will add an estimated $10&#160;billion to Newfoundland and Labrador\'s treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The fishing industry remains an important part of the provincial economy, employing roughly 20,000 and contributing over $440&#160;million to the GDP. The combined harvest of fish such as <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">cod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halibut" title="Halibut">halibut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mackerel" title="Mackerel">mackerel</a> was 150,000&#160;tonnes (165,000&#160;tons) valued at about $130&#160;million in 2006. <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">Shellfish</a>, such as crab, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clams" title="Clams">clams</a>, accounted for 195,000&#160;tonnes (215,000&#160;tons) with a value of $316&#160;million in the same year. The value of products from the <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">seal hunt</a> was $55&#160;million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-econ-103">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a> is a new industry for the province, which in 2006 produced over 10,000&#160;tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_salmon" title="Atlantic salmon">Atlantic salmon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mussels" title="Mussels">mussels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">steelhead trout</a> worth over $50&#160;million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ_103-4"><a href="#cite_note-econ-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Newsprint" title="Newsprint">Newsprint</a> is produced by one <a href="/wiki/Paper_mill" title="Paper mill">paper mill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Corner_Brook" title="Corner Brook">Corner Brook</a> with a capacity of 420,000&#160;tonnes (462,000&#160;tons) per year. The value of newsprint exports varies greatly from year to year, depending on the global market price. Lumber is produced by numerous mills in Newfoundland.\n</p><p>Apart from seafood processing, paper manufacture and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_refining" title="Oil refining">oil refining</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> manufacturing in the province consists of smaller industries producing food,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> brewing and other beverage production.\n</p><p>Agriculture in Newfoundland is limited to areas south of <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="St. John\'s, Newfoundland and Labrador">St. John\'s</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cormack,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Cormack, Newfoundland and Labrador">Cormack</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wooddale,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Wooddale, Newfoundland and Labrador">Wooddale</a>, areas near <a href="/wiki/Musgravetown" title="Musgravetown">Musgravetown</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Codroy_Valley" title="Codroy Valley">Codroy Valley</a>. Potatoes, <a href="/wiki/Rutabaga" title="Rutabaga">rutabagas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnips</a>, carrots and <a href="/wiki/Cabbage" title="Cabbage">cabbage</a> are grown for local consumption. Poultry and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a> are also produced. Wild <a href="/wiki/Blueberry" title="Blueberry">blueberries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaccinium_vitis-idaea" title="Vaccinium vitis-idaea">partridgeberries</a> (lingonberries) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloudberry" title="Cloudberry">bakeapples</a> (cloudberries) are harvested commercially and used in <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">jams</a> and wine making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Dairy production is another huge part of the Newfoundland Agriculture Industry.\n</p><p>Tourism is also a significant contributor to the province\'s economy. In 2006 nearly 500,000 non-resident tourists visited Newfoundland and Labrador, spending an estimated $366&#160;million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ_103-5"><a href="#cite_note-econ-103">[103]</a></sup> Tourism is most popular throughout the months of June&#8211;September, the warmest months of the year with the longest hours of daylight.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Government of Newfoundland and Labrador">Government of Newfoundland and Labrador</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Politics of Newfoundland and Labrador">Politics of Newfoundland and Labrador</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confederation_Building_(front),_St._John%27s,_Newfoundland,_Canada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2799" data-file-width="4199" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Confederation_Building_%28front%29%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland%2C_Canada.jpg/220px-Confederation_Building_%28front%29%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland%2C_Canada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Confederation_Building_%28front%29%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland%2C_Canada.jpg/330px-Confederation_Building_%28front%29%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland%2C_Canada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Confederation_Building_%28front%29%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland%2C_Canada.jpg/440px-Confederation_Building_%28front%29%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland%2C_Canada.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Confederation_Building_(front),_St._John%27s,_Newfoundland,_Canada.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Confederation_Building_(Newfoundland_and_Labrador)" title="Confederation Building (Newfoundland and Labrador)">Confederation Building</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Newfoundland and Labrador is governed by a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary government</a> within the construct of <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_in_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Monarchy in Newfoundland and Labrador">monarchy in Newfoundland and Labrador</a> is the foundation of the executive, <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judicial</a> branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The sovereign is Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>, who also serves as head of state of <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_realm" title="Commonwealth realm">15 other Commonwealth countries</a>, each of Canada\'s nine other provinces and the Canadian federal realm; she resides in the United Kingdom. The Queen\'s representative in Newfoundland and Labrador is the <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Lieutenant Governor of Newfoundland and Labrador">Lieutenant Governor of Newfoundland and Labrador</a>, presently <a href="/wiki/Judy_Foote" title="Judy Foote">Judy Foote</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The direct participation of the royal and viceroyal figures in any of these areas of governance is limited; in practice, their use of the executive powers is directed by <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Executive Council of Newfoundland and Labrador">the Executive Council</a>, a committee of <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_the_Crown" title="Minister of the Crown">ministers of the Crown</a> responsible to the unicameral, elected <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador_House_of_Assembly" title="Newfoundland and Labrador House of Assembly">House of Assembly</a> and chosen and headed by the <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Premier of Newfoundland and Labrador">Premier of Newfoundland and Labrador</a> Dwight Ball the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> To ensure the stability of government, the lieutenant governor will usually appoint as premier the person who is usually the current leader of the political party that can obtain the confidence of a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> in the House of Assembly. The leader of the party with the second-most seats usually becomes the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(Newfoundland_and_Labrador)" title="Leader of the Opposition (Newfoundland and Labrador)">Leader of Her Majesty\'s Loyal Opposition</a> and is part of an adversarial parliamentary system intended to keep the government in check.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Each of the 40 <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador_House_of_Assembly" title="Newfoundland and Labrador House of Assembly">Members of the House of Assembly</a> (MHA) is elected by simple plurality in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_electoral_district" title="Canadian electoral district">electoral district</a>. General elections must be called by the lieutenant governor on the second Tuesday in October four years after the previous election, or may be called, on the advice of the premier, should the government lose a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confidence_vote" title="Confidence vote">confidence vote</a> in the legislature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Traditionally, politics in the province have been dominated by both the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Liberal Party of Newfoundland and Labrador">Liberal Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Conservative_Party_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Progressive Conservative Party of Newfoundland and Labrador">Progressive Conservative Party</a>. However, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador_general_election,_2011" title="Newfoundland and Labrador general election, 2011">2011 provincial election</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Democratic_Party_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="New Democratic Party of Newfoundland and Labrador">New Democratic Party</a>, which had only ever attained minor success, had a major breakthrough and placed second in the popular vote behind the Progressive Conservatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Art of Newfoundland and Labrador">Art of Newfoundland and Labrador</a></div><p>\nBefore 1950, the formal visual arts were a minor aspect of Newfoundland cultural life, compared to the performing arts such as music or theatre. Until about 1900, most art was the work of visiting artists, who included members of the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Seven_(artists)" title="Group of Seven (artists)">Group of Seven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_Kent" title="Rockwell Kent">Rockwell Kent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eliot_O%27Hara" title="Eliot O\'Hara">Eliot O\'Hara</a>. Artists such as Newfoundland-born <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maurice_Galbraith_Cullen" title="Maurice Galbraith Cullen">Maurice Cullen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Pilot" title="Robert Pilot">Robert Pilot</a> travelled to Europe to study art in prominent ateliers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maurice_Cullen._%22A_Misty_Afternoon,_St._John%27s,_Newfoundland.%22_(1910),_oil_on_canvas._122.5_cm_x_153.25._The_Rooms_Provincial_Art_Gallery_Collection,_Gift_of_the_National_Art_Gallery_of_Canada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1439" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Maurice_Cullen._%22A_Misty_Afternoon%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland.%22_%281910%29%2C_oil_on_canvas._122.5_cm_x_153.25._The_Rooms_Provincial_Art_Gallery_Collection%2C_Gift_of_the_National_Art_Gallery_of_Canada.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Maurice_Cullen._%22A_Misty_Afternoon%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland.%22_%281910%29%2C_oil_on_canvas._122.5_cm_x_153.25._The_Rooms_Provincial_Art_Gallery_Collection%2C_Gift_of_the_National_Art_Gallery_of_Canada.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Maurice_Cullen._%22A_Misty_Afternoon%2C_St._John%27s%2C_Newfoundland.%22_%281910%29%2C_oil_on_canvas._122.5_cm_x_153.25._The_Rooms_Provincial_Art_Gallery_Collection%2C_Gift_of_the_National_Art_Gallery_of_Canada.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maurice_Cullen._%22A_Misty_Afternoon,_St._John%27s,_Newfoundland.%22_(1910),_oil_on_canvas._122.5_cm_x_153.25._The_Rooms_Provincial_Art_Gallery_Collection,_Gift_of_the_National_Art_Gallery_of_Canada.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maurice Cullen. "A Misty Afternoon, St. John\'s, Newfoundland." (1910), oil on canvas. 122.5 cm x 153.25. The Rooms Provincial Art Gallery Collection, Gift of the National Art Gallery of Canada</div></div></div>\n<p>By the turn of the century, amateur art was made by people living and working in the province. These artists included J.W. Hayward and his son Thomas B. Hayward, Agnes Marian Ayre, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_B._Goodridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Harold B. Goodridge (page does not exist)">Harold B. Goodridge</a>, the last of whom worked on a number of mural commissions, notably one for the lobby of the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_Building_(Newfoundland_and_Labrador)" title="Confederation Building (Newfoundland and Labrador)">Confederation Building</a> in St. John\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage.nf.ca_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-heritage.nf.ca-119">[119]</a></sup> Local art societies became prominent in the 1940s, particularly <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Art_Students_Club&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Art Students Club (page does not exist)">The Art Students Club</a>, which opened in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Newfoundland and Labrador joined Canada in 1949, government grants fostered a supportive environment for visual artists, primarily painters. The visual arts of the province developed significantly in the second half of the century, with the return of young Newfoundland artists who had studied abroad. Among the first were <a href="/wiki/Rae_Perlin" title="Rae Perlin">Rae Perlin</a>, who studied at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_Students_League" title="Art Students League">Art Students League</a> in New York, and <a href="/wiki/Helen_Parsons_Shepherd" title="Helen Parsons Shepherd">Helen Parsons Shepherd</a> and her husband Reginald Shepherd, who both graduated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontario_College_of_Art" title="Ontario College of Art">Ontario College of Art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage.nf.ca_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-heritage.nf.ca-119">[119]</a></sup> The Shepherds established the province\'s first art school, the Newfoundland Academy of Art, in a home in downtown St. John\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newfoundland-born painter <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Pratt" title="Christopher Pratt">Christopher Pratt</a> returned to the province in 1961 with his wife <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pratt_(painter)" title="Mary Pratt (painter)">Mary Pratt</a> to work at the newly established <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Memorial_University_Art_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Memorial University Art Gallery (page does not exist)">Memorial University Art Gallery</a> as its first curator, later transitioning to painting full-time in Salmonier. Wesleyville\'s <a href="/wiki/David_Blackwood" title="David Blackwood">David Blackwood</a> graduated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontario_College_of_Art" title="Ontario College of Art">Ontario College of Art</a> in the early 1960s and achieved acclaim with his images of Newfoundland culture and history, though he no longer resides in the province. Newfoundland-born artist <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Squires" title="Gerald Squires">Gerald Squires</a> returned in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage.nf.ca_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-heritage.nf.ca-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The creation of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Memorial_University_Extension_Services&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Memorial University Extension Services (page does not exist)">The Memorial University Extension Services</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=St._Michael%27s_Printshop&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="St. Michael\'s Printshop (page does not exist)">St. Michael\'s Printshop</a> in the 1960s and 1970s attracted a number of visual artist to the province to teach and create art. Similarly, the school in Hibbs Hole (now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hibb%27s_Cove" title="Hibb\'s Cove">Hibb\'s Cove</a>), established by painter <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=George_Noseworthy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="George Noseworthy (page does not exist)">George Noseworthy</a>, brought professional artists such as <a href="/wiki/Anne_Meredith_Barry" title="Anne Meredith Barry">Anne Meredith Barry</a> to the province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> A notable artist during this period is <a href="/wiki/Marlene_Creates" title="Marlene Creates">Marlene Creates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heritage.nf.ca_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-heritage.nf.ca-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1980 to present, opportunities for artists continued to develop, as galleries such as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Art_Gallery_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Art Gallery of Newfoundland and Labrador (page does not exist)">Art Gallery of Newfoundland and Labrador</a> (which later became <a href="/wiki/The_Rooms" title="The Rooms">The Rooms</a> Provincial Art Gallery), the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Resource_Centre_for_the_Arts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Resource Centre for the Arts (page does not exist)">Resource Centre for the Arts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Edge" title="Eastern Edge">Eastern Edge</a> were established. Fine arts education programs were established at post-secondary institutions such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Wilfred_Grenfell_College" title="Sir Wilfred Grenfell College">Sir Wilfred Grenfell College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Corner_Brook" title="Corner Brook">Corner Brook</a>, the Western Community College (now <a href="/wiki/College_of_the_North_Atlantic" title="College of the North Atlantic">College of the North Atlantic</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Stephenville,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Stephenville, Newfoundland and Labrador">Stephenville</a>, and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Anna_Templeton_Centre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Anna Templeton Centre (page does not exist)">Anna Templeton Centre</a> in St. John\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newfoundland and Labrador\'s arts community is recognized nationally and internationally. The creation of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fogo_Island_Arts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fogo Island Arts (page does not exist)">Fogo Island Arts</a> in 2008 on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fogo_Island,_Newfoundland" title="Fogo Island, Newfoundland">Fogo Island</a> created a residency-based contemporary art program for artists, filmmakers, writers, musicians, curators, designers, and thinkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In 2013 and 2015, the province was represented at the <a href="/wiki/Venice_Biennale" title="Venice Biennale">Venice Biennale</a> as Official Collateral Projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Philippa_Jones" title="Philippa Jones">Philippa Jones</a> became the first Newfoundland and Labrador artist to be included in the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Canada" title="National Gallery of Canada">National Gallery of Canada</a> contemporary art biennial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Other notable contemporary artists who have received national and international attention include <a href="/wiki/Will_Gill" title="Will Gill">Will Gill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kym_Greeley" title="Kym Greeley">Kym Greeley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ned_Pratt" title="Ned Pratt">Ned Pratt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Wilkins" title="Peter Wilkins">Peter Wilkins</a>.\n</p><p>As of 2011, a study documented approximately 1,200 artists, representing 0.47% of the province\'s labour force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Music of Newfoundland and Labrador">Music of Newfoundland and Labrador</a></div>\n<p>Newfoundland and Labrador has a folk musical heritage based on the <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Ireland" title="Music of Ireland">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_folk_music" title="English folk music">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Scotland" title="Music of Scotland">Scottish</a> traditions that were brought to its shores centuries ago. Though similar in its <a href="/wiki/Celtic_music" title="Celtic music">Celtic</a> influence to neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>, Newfoundland and Labrador are more Irish than Scottish, and have more elements imported from English and French music than those provinces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>. Much of the region\'s music focuses on the strong seafaring tradition in the area, and includes <a href="/wiki/Sea_shanty" title="Sea shanty">sea shanties</a> and other sailing songs. Some modern traditional musicians include <a href="/wiki/Great_Big_Sea" title="Great Big Sea">Great Big Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Ennis_Sisters" title="The Ennis Sisters">The Ennis Sisters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dardanelles" title="The Dardanelles">The Dardanelles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Hynes" title="Ron Hynes">Ron Hynes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Payne_(folk_singer)" title="Jim Payne (folk singer)">Jim Payne</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_Symphony_Orchestra" title="Newfoundland Symphony Orchestra">Newfoundland Symphony Orchestra</a> began in St John\'s in 1962 as a 20-piece string orchestra known as the St. John\'s Orchestra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Principals from this form a string quartet which performs regularly. A school of music at <a href="/wiki/Memorial_University_of_Newfoundland" title="Memorial University of Newfoundland">Memorial University</a> schedules a variety of concerts and has a chamber orchestra and jazz band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Two members of its faculty, Nancy Dahn on violin and Timothy Steeves on piano, perform as Duo Concertante<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> and are responsible for establishing an annual music festival in August, the <a href="/wiki/Tuckamore_Festival" title="Tuckamore Festival">Tuckamore Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Both the school of music and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Opera_on_the_Avalon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Opera on the Avalon (page does not exist)">Opera on the Avalon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> produce operatic works. Memorial\'s <a class="external text" href="https://www.mun.ca/mmap/" rel="nofollow">Research Centre for the Study of Music, Media, and Place</a>, houses Memorial\'s graduate program in ethnomusicology. A leading institution for research in ethnomusicology, the Centre offers academic lectures, scholarly residencies, conferences, symposia, and outreach activities to the province on music and culture.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_in_fiction" title="Newfoundland in fiction">Newfoundland in fiction</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prose_fiction">Prose fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Prose fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Duley" title="Margaret Duley">Margaret Duley</a> (1894&#8211;1968) was arguably Newfoundland\'s first novelist to gain an international audience. Amongst her works are <i>The Eyes of the Gull</i> (1936), <i>Cold Pastoral</i> (1939) and <i>Highway to Valour</i> (1941).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Subsequent important novelists include <a href="/wiki/Harold_Horwood" title="Harold Horwood">Harold Horwood</a>, who wrote <i>Tomorrow Will Be Sunday</i> (1966) and <i>White Eskimo</i> (1972), and <a href="/wiki/Percy_Janes" title="Percy Janes">Percy Janes</a>, author of <i>House of Hate</i> (1970).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Notable contemporary novelists include <a href="/wiki/Michael_Crummey" title="Michael Crummey">Michael Crummey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Johnston_(writer)" title="Wayne Johnston (writer)">Wayne Johnston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Moore_(writer)" title="Lisa Moore (writer)">Lisa Moore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_J._Harvey" title="Kenneth J. Harvey">Kenneth J. Harvey</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Author_Michael_Crummey,_May_28_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Author_Michael_Crummey%2C_May_28_2014.jpg/150px-Author_Michael_Crummey%2C_May_28_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Author_Michael_Crummey%2C_May_28_2014.jpg/225px-Author_Michael_Crummey%2C_May_28_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Author_Michael_Crummey%2C_May_28_2014.jpg/300px-Author_Michael_Crummey%2C_May_28_2014.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Author_Michael_Crummey,_May_28_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Michael_Crummey" title="Michael Crummey">Michael Crummey</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Crummey\'s debut novel, <i>River Thieves</i> (2001), became a Canadian bestseller and won the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Head_Raddall_Award" title="Thomas Head Raddall Award">Thomas Head Raddall Award</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Winterset_Award" title="Winterset Award">Winterset Award</a> for Excellence in Newfoundland Writing, and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Independent_Booksellers%27_Choice_Award&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Atlantic Independent Booksellers\' Choice Award (page does not exist)">Atlantic Independent Booksellers\' Choice Award</a>. It was also shortlisted for the <a href="/wiki/Giller_Prize" title="Giller Prize">Giller Prize</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Writers%27_Prize" title="Commonwealth Writers\' Prize">Commonwealth Writers\' Prize</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Books_in_Canada_First_Novel_Award" title="Books in Canada First Novel Award">Books in Canada First Novel Award</a>, as well as being longlisted for the <a href="/wiki/International_Dublin_Literary_Award" title="International Dublin Literary Award">International Dublin Literary Award</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Encyclopaedia_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Encyclopaedia-136">[136]</a></sup> His second novel, <i>The Wreckage</i> (2005), was longlisted for the 2007 International Dublin Literary Award, and his third novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Galore_(novel)" title="Galore (novel)">Galore</a></i> (2009), was shortlisted for the 2011 International Dublin Literary Award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Encyclopaedia_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Encyclopaedia-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Johnston\'s fiction deals primarily with the province of Newfoundland and Labrador, often in a historical setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> His first novel, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Story_of_Bobby_O%27Malley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Story of Bobby O\'Malley (page does not exist)">The Story of Bobby O\'Malley</a></i>, brought him early critical notice and won the W.H. Smith/Books in Canada First Novel Award in 1985. His second novel, <i>The Time of Their Lives</i>, won the Air Canada/Canadian Authors Association Award for Most Promising Young Canadian Writer in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2017)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> His novel <i>The Divine Ryans</i> won the 1991 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Head_Raddall_Award" title="Thomas Head Raddall Award">Thomas Head Raddall Award</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> and was subsequently adapted for the screen. His breakthrough novel, 1998\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Colony_of_Unrequited_Dreams" title="The Colony of Unrequited Dreams">The Colony of Unrequited Dreams</a></i> &#8211; shortlisted for both the Giller Prize and the Governor General\'s Award for fiction &#8211; was acclaimed for its historical portrayal of legendary Newfoundland politician <a href="/wiki/Joey_Smallwood" title="Joey Smallwood">Joey Smallwood</a>. It was featured on the cover of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times_Book_Review" title="New York Times Book Review">New York Times Book Review</a></i> when it was released in the United States, and was an international best seller.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Colony_of_Unrequited_Dreams" title="The Colony of Unrequited Dreams">The Colony of Unrequited Dreams</a></i> won or was nominated for sixteen national and international awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> In 2002, Johnston published <i>The Navigator of New York</i>, which was shortlisted for both the Giller Prize and the Governor General\'s Award for fiction. Johnston was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Writers%27_Trust_Engel/Findley_Award" title="Writers\' Trust Engel/Findley Award">Writers\' Trust Engel/Findley Award </a> in recognition of his contribution to Canadian Literature in 2011. Several of Johnston\'s books have been published in the United States, the United Kingdom, the Netherlands, Germany and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Harvey is a novelist whose books are published throughout the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Amongst these are <i>The Town That Forgot How to Breathe</i> (2003), which won the 2004 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Head_Raddall_Award" title="Thomas Head Raddall Award">Thomas Head Raddall Award</a> and Italy\'s Libro del Mare), <i>Inside</i> (2006), long listed for the <a href="/wiki/Giller_Prize" title="Giller Prize">Giller Prize</a>), winner of the <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Writers%27_Trust_Fiction_Prize" title="Rogers Writers\' Trust Fiction Prize">Rogers Writers\' Trust Fiction Prize</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Winterset_Award" title="Winterset Award">Winterset Award</a>, and <i>Blackstrap Hawco</i> (2008), which was longlisted for the <a href="/wiki/Giller_Prize" title="Giller Prize">Giller Prize</a>), and shortlisted for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Writers_Prize" title="Commonwealth Writers Prize">Commonwealth Writers Prize</a>.\n</p><p>Moore\'s first two books, <i>Degrees of Nakedness</i> (1995) and <i>Open</i> (2002), are short-story collections. <i>Open</i> was a commercial and critical breakthrough, earning a nomination for the <a href="/wiki/Giller_Prize" title="Giller Prize">Giller Prize</a>. Her first novel, <i>Alligator</i> (2005), was also nominated for the Giller Prize. It won the 2006 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Writers%27_Prize" title="Commonwealth Writers\' Prize">Commonwealth Writers\' Prize</a> Best Book Award, Caribbean and Canada Region, and was longlisted for the 2007 <a href="/wiki/International_Dublin_Literary_Award" title="International Dublin Literary Award">International Dublin Literary Award</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Moore often incorporates her Newfoundland heritage in her work; <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alligator_(novel)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alligator (novel) (page does not exist)">Alligator</a></i>, for example, is set in St. John\'s.\nFebruary tells the story of Helen O\'Mara, who lost her husband Cal when the oil rig, <i><a href="/wiki/Ocean_Ranger" title="Ocean Ranger">Ocean Ranger</a></i>, sank off the coast of Newfoundland during a Valentine\'s Day storm in 1982. Moore was one of 13 novelists on the long list for the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Booker_Prize" title="Booker Prize">Booker Prize</a> for her novel <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=February_(novel)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="February (novel) (page does not exist)">February</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The earliest works of poetry in <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a>, mainly written by visitors and targeted at a European audience, described the new territories in optimistic terms. One of the first works was Robert Hayman\'s <i>Quodlibets</i>, a collection of verses composed in Newfoundland and published in 1628.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="375" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG/200px-Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG/300px-Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edwin_J._Pratt.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poet <a href="/wiki/E._J._Pratt" title="E. J. Pratt">E. J. Pratt</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Newfoundland poet <a href="/wiki/E._J._Pratt" title="E. J. Pratt">E. J. Pratt</a> described the struggle to make a living from the sea in poems about maritime life and the history of Canada. In 1923, his first commercial poetry collection, <i>Newfoundland Verse</i>, was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-online-144">[144]</a></sup> It is frequently archaic in diction, and reflects a <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">pietistic</a> and late-<a href="/wiki/Romantic_poetry" title="Romantic poetry">Romantic</a> lyrical sensibility. The collection has humorous and sympathetic portraits of Newfoundland characters, and creates an elegiac mood in poems concerning sea tragedies or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_War" title="Great War">Great War</a> losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gingell_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-gingell-145">[145]</a></sup> With illustrations by <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Seven_(artists)" title="Group of Seven (artists)">Group of Seven</a> member <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Varley" title="Frederick Varley">Frederick Varley</a>, <i>Newfoundland Verse</i> proved to be Pratt\'s "breakthrough collection." He went on to publish 18 more books of poetry in his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> "Recognition came with the narrative poems <i>The Witches&#8217; Brew</i> (1925), <i>Titans</i> (1926), and <i>The Roosevelt and the Antinoe</i> (1930), and though he published a substantial body of lyric verse, it is as a narrative poet that Pratt is remembered." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vulpe_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-vulpe-147">[147]</a></sup> Pratt\'s poetry "frequently reflects his Newfoundland background, though specific references to it appear in relatively few poems, mostly in <i>Newfoundland Verse</i>", says <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Encyclopedia" title="Canadian Encyclopedia">The Canadian Encyclopedia</a></i>. "But the sea and maritime life are central to many of his poems, for example, "The Cachalot" (1926), which describes duels between a whale and its foes, a <a href="/wiki/Giant_squid" title="Giant squid">giant squid</a> and a whaling ship and crew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pitt_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-pitt-148">[148]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Amongst more recent poets are <a href="/wiki/Tom_Dawe" title="Tom Dawe">Tom Dawe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Pittman" title="Al Pittman">Al Pittman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Dalton" title="Mary Dalton">Mary Dalton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Walsh" title="Agnes Walsh">Agnes Walsh</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Patrick_Warner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Patrick Warner (page does not exist)">Patrick Warner</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Steffler" title="John Steffler">John Steffler</a>. Canadian poet <a href="/wiki/Don_McKay" title="Don McKay">Don McKay</a> has resided in St John\'s in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Playwrights">Playwrights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Playwrights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>"1967 marked the opening of the St. John\'s Arts and Culture Centre and the first all-Canadian Dominion Drama Festival. Playwrights across Canada began writing, and this explosion was also felt in Newfoundland and Labrador. Subregional festivals saw Newfoundland plays compete - <i>Wreaker</i>s by <a href="/wiki/Cassie_Brown" title="Cassie Brown">Cassie Brown</a>, <i>Tomorrow Will Be Sunday</i> by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tom_Cahill_(writer)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tom Cahill (writer) (page does not exist)">Tom Cahill</a>, and <i>Holdin\' Ground</i> by <a href="/wiki/Ted_Russell_(Canadian_politician)" title="Ted Russell (Canadian politician)">Ted Russell</a>. Cahill\'s play went on to receive top honours and a performance at <a href="/wiki/Expo_67" title="Expo 67">Expo 67</a> in Montreal. Joining Brown and Cahill in the seventies were <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cook_(playwright)" title="Michael Cook (playwright)">Michael Cook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al_Pittman" title="Al Pittman">Al Pittman</a>, both prolific writers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages_spoken_in_the_Province">Languages spoken in the Province</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Languages spoken in the Province">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_English" title="Newfoundland English">Newfoundland English</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland_Irish" title="Newfoundland Irish">Newfoundland Irish</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_English" title="Newfoundland English">Newfoundland English</a> is any of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accent_(dialect)" title="Accent (dialect)">accents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a> of <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> found in the province of Newfoundland and Labrador. Most of these differ substantially from the English commonly spoken elsewhere in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" title="North Atlantic">North Atlantic</a>.  Many Newfoundland dialects are similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Country_dialects" title="West Country dialects">West Country dialects</a> of the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a> in England, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_Bristol" title="City of Bristol">city of Bristol</a> and counties <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a>, while others resemble dialects of Ireland\'s southeast, particularly <a href="/wiki/County_Waterford" title="County Waterford">Waterford</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Wexford" title="County Wexford">Wexford</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Kilkenny" title="County Kilkenny">Kilkenny</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">Cork</a>. Still others blend elements of both, and there is also a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> influence on the dialects. While the Scots came in smaller numbers than the English and Irish, they had a large influence on Newfoundland society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renamed_from_2006_on_20151223002828_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-renamed_from_2006_on_20151223002828-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Short_History_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Short_History-154">[154]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newfoundland was also the only place outside Europe to have its own distinct name in <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i>Talamh an &#201;isc</i>, which means \'land of the fish\'. The Irish language is now extinct in Newfoundland. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scots_Gaelic" title="Scots Gaelic">Scots Gaelic</a> was also once spoken in the southwest of Newfoundland, following the settlement there, from the middle of the 19th century, of small numbers of Gaelic-speaking Scots from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Breton,_Nova_Scotia" title="Cape Breton, Nova Scotia">Cape Breton, Nova Scotia</a>. Some 150 years later, the language has not entirely disappeared, although it has no fluent speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A vestigial community of French speakers exists on Newfoundland\'s <a href="/wiki/Port_au_Port_Peninsula" title="Port au Port Peninsula">Port au Port Peninsula</a>; a remnant of the "<a href="/wiki/French_Shore" title="French Shore">French Shore</a>" along the island\'s west coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heritage_Newfoundland_and_Labrador_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heritage_Newfoundland_and_Labrador-156">[156]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several aboriginal languages are spoken in the Province, representing the <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_languages" title="Algonquian languages">Algonquian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Beothuk" title="Beothuk">Beothuk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mi%27kmaq_language" title="Mi\'kmaq language">Mi\'kmaq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Innu" title="Innu">Innu</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eskimo-Aleut" title="Eskimo-Aleut">Eskimo-Aleut</a> (<a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Inuktitut</a>) language families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heritage_Newfoundland_and_Labrador_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heritage_Newfoundland_and_Labrador-156">[156]</a></sup>\n</p><p><b>See</b> <a class="external text" href="http://www.heritage.nf.ca/dictionary/" rel="nofollow"><i>Dictionary of Newfoundland English</i>, 2d ed</a>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Newfoundland and Labrador has a somewhat different sports culture from the rest of Canada, owing in part to its long history separate from the rest of Canada and under British rule. <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">Ice hockey</a>, however, remains popular; a minor league professional team called the <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_Growlers" title="Newfoundland Growlers">Newfoundland Growlers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/ECHL" title="ECHL">ECHL</a> plays at the <a href="/wiki/Mile_One_Centre" title="Mile One Centre">Mile One Centre</a> in St. John\'s since the 2018&#8211;19 season. The area had an intermittent <a href="/wiki/American_Hockey_League" title="American Hockey League">American Hockey League</a> presence with the <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_Maple_Leafs" title="St. John\'s Maple Leafs">St. John\'s Maple Leafs</a> then <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_IceCaps" title="St. John\'s IceCaps">St. John\'s IceCaps</a> until 2017, and the <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_Senior_Hockey_League" title="Newfoundland Senior Hockey League">Newfoundland Senior Hockey League</a> has teams around the island. Since the departure of the <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_Fog_Devils" title="St. John\'s Fog Devils">St. John\'s Fog Devils</a> in 2008, Newfoundland and Labrador is the only province in Canada to not have a team in the major junior <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Hockey_League" title="Canadian Hockey League">Canadian Hockey League</a> (should one ever join it would be placed in the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Major_Junior_Hockey_League" title="Quebec Major Junior Hockey League">QMJHL</a>, which hosted the Fog Devils and has jurisdiction over Atlantic Canada).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Association football</a> (soccer) and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> are both more popular in Newfoundland and Labrador than the rest of Canada in general. Soccer is hosted at <a href="/wiki/King_George_V_Park" title="King George V Park">King George V Park</a>, a 6,000-seat stadium built as Newfoundland\'s national stadium during the time as an independent dominion. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swilers_Rugby_Park" title="Swilers Rugby Park">Swilers Rugby Park</a> is home of the <a href="/wiki/Swilers_RFC" title="Swilers RFC">Swilers RFC</a> rugby union club, as well as <a href="/wiki/The_Rock_(rugby_team)" title="The Rock (rugby team)">The Rock</a>, one of the four regional teams in the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Rugby_Championship" title="Canadian Rugby Championship">Canadian Rugby Championship</a>. Other sports facilities in Newfoundland and Labrador include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pepsi_Centre" title="Pepsi Centre">Pepsi Centre</a>, an indoor arena in Corner Brook; <a href="/wiki/Shamrock_Field" title="Shamrock Field">Shamrock Field</a>, Canada\'s national Gaelic Games venue in St. John\'s; and <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick%27s_Park" title="St. Patrick\'s Park">St. Patrick\'s Park</a>, a baseball park in St. John\'s.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gridiron_football" title="Gridiron football">Gridiron football</a>, be it either <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American</a> or <a href="/wiki/Canadian_football" title="Canadian football">Canadian</a>, is almost nonexistent; it is the only Canadian province other than <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a> to have never hosted a <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Football_League" title="Canadian Football League">Canadian Football League</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIS_football" title="CIS football">Canadian Interuniversity Sport</a> game, and it was not until 2013 that the province saw its first amateur teams form.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">Cricket</a> was once a popular sport. The earliest mention is in the <i>Newfoundland Mercantile Journal</i>, Thursday September 16, 1824, indicating that the St. John&#8217;s Cricket Club was an established club at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The St. John&#8217;s Cricket club was one of the first cricket clubs in North America. Other centres were at <a href="/wiki/Harbour_Grace" title="Harbour Grace">Harbour Grace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twillingate" title="Twillingate">Twillingate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trinity,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Trinity, Newfoundland and Labrador">Trinity</a>. The heyday of the game was the late nineteenth and early twentieth century, at which time there was league in St. John&#8217;s, as well as an interschool tournament. John Shannon Munn is Newfoundland\'s most famous cricketer, having represented Oxford University. After the first World War, cricket declined in popularity and was replaced by soccer and baseball. However, with the arrival of immigrants from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>,\ncricket is once again gaining interest in the province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newfoundland_and_Labrador&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bridges_in_Canada" title="List of bridges in Canada">List of bridges in Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Newfoundland_and_Labrador_highways" title="List of Newfoundland and Labrador highways">List of Newfoundland and Labrador highways</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="List of airports in Newfoundland and Labrador">List of airports in Newfoundland and Labrador</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="1302" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg/220px-Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg/330px-Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg/440px-Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kings_Cove_Head_Lighthouse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King\'s Cove Head lighthouse in <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Cove" title="King\'s Cove">King\'s Cove</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Within the province, the Newfoundland and Labrador Department of Transportation and Works operates or sponsors 15 automobile, passenger and freight ferry routes which connect various communities along the province\'s significant coastline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A regular passenger and car ferry service, lasting about 90 minutes, crosses the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Belle_Isle" title="Strait of Belle Isle">Strait of Belle Isle</a>, connecting the province\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_of_Newfoundland" title="Island of Newfoundland">island of Newfoundland</a> with the region of <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a> on the mainland. The ferry <i><a href="/wiki/MV_Apollo" title="MV Apollo">MV Apollo</a></i> travels from <a href="/wiki/St._Barbe,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="St. Barbe, Newfoundland and Labrador">St. Barbe, Newfoundland</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_Peninsula" title="Great Northern Peninsula">Great Northern Peninsula</a> to the port town of <a href="/wiki/Blanc-Sablon,_Quebec" title="Blanc-Sablon, Quebec">Blanc-Sablon, Quebec</a>, located on the provincial border and beside the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%27Anse-au-Clair,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="L\'Anse-au-Clair, Newfoundland and Labrador">L\'Anse-au-Clair, Labrador</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> The <i>MV Sir Robert Bond</i> once provided seasonal ferry service between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewisporte,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Lewisporte, Newfoundland and Labrador">Lewisporte</a> on the island and the towns of <a href="/wiki/Cartwright,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Cartwright, Newfoundland and Labrador">Cartwright</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Happy_Valley%E2%80%93Goose_Bay" title="Happy Valley&#8211;Goose Bay">Happy Valley&#8211;Goose Bay</a> in Labrador, but has not run since the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Labrador_Highway" title="Trans-Labrador Highway">Trans-Labrador Highway</a> in 2010, allowing access from <a href="/wiki/Blanc-Sablon,_Quebec" title="Blanc-Sablon, Quebec">Blanc-Sablon, Quebec</a>, to major parts of Labrador.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Several smaller ferries connect numerous other coastal towns and offshore island communities around the island of Newfoundland and up the Labrador coast as far north as <a href="/wiki/Nain,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Nain, Newfoundland and Labrador">Nain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Inter-provincial ferry services are provided by <a href="/wiki/Marine_Atlantic" title="Marine Atlantic">Marine Atlantic</a>, a federal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_corporation" title="Crown corporation">Crown corporation</a> which operates auto-passenger ferries from <a href="/wiki/North_Sydney,_Nova_Scotia" title="North Sydney, Nova Scotia">North Sydney</a>, Nova Scotia, to the towns of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Channel-Port_aux_Basques,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Channel-Port aux Basques, Newfoundland and Labrador">Port aux Basques</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argentia,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Argentia, Newfoundland and Labrador">Argentia</a> on the southern coast of Newfoundland island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_International_Airport" title="St. John\'s International Airport">St. John\'s International Airport</a> YYT and the <a href="/wiki/Gander_International_Airport" title="Gander International Airport">Gander International Airport</a> YQX are the only airports in the province that are part of the <a href="/wiki/National_Airports_System" title="National Airports System">National Airports System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The St. John\'s International Airport handles nearly 1,200,000 passengers a year making it the busiest airport in the province and the eleventh busiest airport in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> The airport is currently undergoing a major expansion of the terminal building which is scheduled to be complete in 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Deer_Lake_Regional_Airport" title="Deer Lake Regional Airport">Deer Lake Airport</a> YDF handles over 300,000 passengers a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Provincial_symbols">Provincial symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
